Justice and Diplomacy: The Caliph as Judge and Interfaith Leader
Nasafi details the duties of adjudication and interfaith relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the caliph's duties in adjudicating disputes and managing relations with non-Muslims, and the moral qualities required for these tasks.

Core Idea
The caliph must be a paragon of justice, asceticism, and moral integrity to handle disputes and interfaith relations fairly.

Imam Nasafi continues his enumeration of the caliph's responsibilities by turning to two highly sensitive areas: resolving injustices and disputes between people, and managing relations with non-Muslim communities. These tasks require the caliph to be completely sincere to Allah, ascetic in worldly matters, and protective of his honor. He also cites a Qur'anic verse commanding justice even toward enemies, underscoring the necessity of impartiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two 'ثم' clauses: one for injustices, one for interfaith matters.
2
Analyze the first duty: injustices
Identify the required qualities: sincere counsel, asceticism, and honor protection. Reflect on why these are needed.
3
Analyze the second duty: interfaith relations
Note the danger of bias and the Qur'anic command to be just. Identify the additional qualities needed: chastity, righteousness, and magnifying Allah's blessings.
4
Combine: the integrated ideal
The author lists many qualities: self-discipline, social skills, insight into politics, and noble character. Understand that these form a unified moral vision for leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الخلافة أمر يتصل به مصالح الدنيا والآخرة يبتلى صاحبها بالأخلاق المختلفة التي لا يصير لها ، ولا يقوم بحفظ حدود الله تعالى مع جميع أهلها إلا من اتسع صدره وظهرت صحبته أصناف البشر ، وعرف معاملة كل نوع على ما عليه حدود الله تعالى في أمثالهم، ثم يتصل به حقوق الله تعالى في الأموال والأبضاع لا يقوم بوفائها إلا من عظم ورعه وتم تقواه وكرم خلقه. ثم يتصل به أحكام الله تعالى لا يصلح لوفائها إلا بأمرين: أحدهما بالتبصر في فنون الحكمة والعلم بأحكام الدين. والثاني: أن لا يبالي بما يناله فى ذات الله تعالى، ولا يخاف لومة لائمة فيما يرجو فيه مرضاة الله تعالى. ثم يتصل به أمور المظالم ومنازعات تقع بين الخلق لا يقدم المرء بوفائها إلا من بالغ في النصح الله تعالى، وتم زهده في الدنيا وظهرت صيانته للعرض، ثم تصل به الأمور التي بين أهل دين الله وغيرهم مما كان في الأمر بذلك مخالفة الدين والموافقة وهما وجهان يدعون إلى الميل والإيثار وإلى الجور والظلم، ولذلك قال الله عز وجل: ( ولا يجرمنكم شنآن قوم على ألا تعدلوا اعدلوا ) الآية. ثم في القريب والبعيد وذلك يدعو إلى ما ذكرت، فيحتاج في ذلك إلى عفيف صالح يعظم في عينه قدر نعم الله تعالى، ويجل في قلبه قدر حقه ليقوم بوفاء ذلك مع أمور في ذلك يحتاج المرء فيه أن يجمع مع العلم بأحكام الله تعالى والقيام بأمور دينه أنواع آداب النفس والمعاشرة، والصحبة والبصيرة في أمر الملك والسياسة ومكارم الأخلاق في حق المعاملة وغير ذلك، لذلك شرط مع وجود الموافقة لما ذكر في الجنس، فإن أكثر تلك الأمور إنما تكون بالاقتداء بقومه والأخذ عن المربين له والتقويم من الذين بهم نشوءه وبهم يعرف أسباب السؤدد فبين النبي عليه السلام القوم الذين في جملتهم وجود لكل نوع من ذلك ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويتصل به أيضا أمر جهاد الأعداء مع حسن الدعوة مع اتصال أنواع الأموال التي تقع في الأيدي تحت طلب أربابها ، والصرف إلى المستحقين لها مما يخاف تلف ذلك لولا رعايتها وحسن النظر، فهي على ما ظهر من احتواء الناس على الأموال وشدة الحرص على أخذها، والله أعلم.
Then the principle is that the caliphate is a matter connected with the interests of this world and the Hereafter. Its holder is tested by various dispositions which he cannot bear, and none can uphold the limits of Allah Almighty among all its people except one whose heart is spacious, whose companionship with all kinds of people is manifest, who knows how to treat each type according to the limits of Allah Almighty in their like. Then connected to it are the rights of Allah Almighty concerning wealth and sexual relations; none can fulfill them except one whose piety is great, whose taqwa is complete, and whose character is generous. Then connected to it are the rulings of Allah Almighty; none is fit to fulfill them except by two things: one is insight into the types of wisdom and knowledge of the rulings of the religion; the second is that he should not care about what befalls him for the sake of Allah Almighty, nor fear the blame of any blamer in what he hopes for the pleasure of Allah Almighty. Then connected to it are matters of injustices and disputes that occur among people; none undertakes to fulfill them except one who is devoted in giving sincere counsel to Allah Almighty, whose asceticism in this world is complete, and whose protection of honor is manifest. Then connected to it are matters between the people of Allah's religion and others, in which there is opposition or agreement with religion, both of which call to inclination and favoritism or to injustice and oppression. For that reason, Allah Almighty said: 'And let not the hatred of a people incite you not to act equitably; act equitably' [5:8]. Then in near and far relations, that calls to what I mentioned. So in that, there is need for a chaste, righteous person who regards the blessings of Allah Almighty as great in his eyes, and who magnifies the right of Allah Almighty in his heart, so that he may fulfill that, along with matters in which a person needs to combine, with knowledge of the rulings of Allah Almighty and carrying out the affairs of His religion, the types of self-discipline, social interaction, companionship, insight into rulership and politics, noble character in dealings, and other things. Therefore, the condition of agreement with what was mentioned was stipulated regarding the lineage, because most of those matters are only achieved by following one's people, taking from one's educators, and rectification by those through whom his upbringing [occurs] and through whom he knows the causes of leadership. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated the people in whose totality exists every type of that, and there is no power except through Allah. And connected to it also is the matter of jihad against enemies with good invitation, along with the connection of the types of wealth that come into hands under the claim of their owners, and spending it to those entitled to it, from which loss is feared were it not for careful management and good supervision. So it is as appears from people's greed for wealth and intense desire to take it, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 1115

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive exposition of the caliph's duties and necessary attributes. The author (Imam Nasafi) explains that the caliphate involves the interests (masalih) of both this world and the Hereafter. The caliph must navigate diverse dispositions and uphold Allah's limits; therefore he needs a broad heart, the ability to interact with all kinds of people, and knowledge of how to treat each according to Islamic law. Then the author lists specific responsibilities: first, the rights of Allah regarding wealth and sexual relations (e.g., zakat, hudud), requiring strong piety (wara') and taqwa as well as generosity. Second, the rulings of Allah (ahkam), requiring two things: insight (tabassur) into wisdom and knowledge of religion, and fearlessness for the sake of Allah, not caring about blame. Third, matters of injustices (mazalim) and disputes, requiring sincere counsel, asceticism (zuhd), and protection of honor. Fourth, relations with non-Muslims, which can lead to bias or injustice, so the caliph must be chaste and righteous, valuing Allah's blessings. The author then notes that these qualities are often acquired through upbringing and association with one's tribe, which is why lineage (nasab) is a condition — and the Prophet (peace be upon him) pointed to Quraysh as embodying those traits. Finally, the caliph must manage jihad and wealth distribution, requiring vigilance against greed. The passage is a kalam argument showing that the Quraysh condition has rational backing.

Key Terms
المظالم
الزهد
الصيانة
العفاف
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A caliph may favor Muslims over non-Muslims in judicial matters.
Correction: The Qur'anic verse and the author's argument insist on absolute justice, even when dealing with those who may oppose Islam.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what quality is necessary for the caliph to fulfill matters of injustices?
A) Extensive wealth
B) Sincere counsel to Allah
C) Popularity
D) Military strength

Key Takeaway
The caliph must embody justice and moral integrity in all disputes, even with non-Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when you hold any position of authority (parent, teacher, leader), do you examine yourself for the qualities of sincere counsel to Allah, asceticism, and protection of honor? Which one do you lack and what will you do about it today?
?
The Qur'an commands justice even if hatred exists (5:8). Is there someone you dislike or disagree with that you treat less fairly? How will you change that starting this week?
?
The caliph must combine knowledge with self-discipline and noble character. What is one area of your life where your character does not match your knowledge? What step will you take to align them?

The Condition of Quraysh and the Duty of Jihad
Nasafi justifies the Quraysh condition and explains the caliph's role in jihad and finance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lineage (specifically Quraysh) was stipulated as a condition for the caliphate, and the caliph's duty in managing jihad and state wealth.

Core Idea
The Prophet's choice of Quraysh as the source of caliphal leadership is rationally justified by the need for upbringing and example in acquiring essential qualities.

In this final part of the passage, Imam Nasafi addresses two related topics: the requirement that the caliph be from Quraysh, and the management of jihad and public funds. He argues that the qualities needed for the caliphate are typically acquired through association with one's tribe and educators, which is why the Prophet indicated Quraysh as the lineage that embodies those traits. He then emphasizes the importance of careful supervision of wealth and the conduct of jihad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical connection between the previous duties and the lineage condition.
2
Understand the lineage argument
The author states that most of the required qualities are acquired through following one's people and being raised by them. This is why lineage (nasab) is a condition, and the Prophet pointed to Quraysh.
This is a rational justification (ta'lil) for a transmitted condition.
3
Analyze the duty of jihad
Jihad requires good invitation and careful management of wealth and spending. Note the concern about greed and loss of funds.
4
Synthesize: ethics of leadership
All the duties lead to a comprehensive vision of leadership that is both religious and practical.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The requirement of Quraysh lineage is an arbitrary tribal privilege.

Why does Nasafi stipulate the condition of lineage (Quraysh) for the caliphate?
A) Because the Prophet exclusively chose Quraysh without reason
B) Because most essential qualities are acquired through upbringing by one's people, and Quraysh exemplify them
C) Because only Quraysh can lead prayers
D) Because Quraysh are wealthier

Key Takeaway
The caliphate requires both noble lineage for proper upbringing and the ability to manage jihad and public funds with care.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The caliph's qualities are developed through following one's people and educators. Who are the people and educators currently shaping your character? Are they leading you toward the qualities needed for responsibility in Allah's sight? What step will you take today to seek better role models?
?
The passage warns of loss due to greed and emphasizes careful supervision of resources. Think of a resource Allah has entrusted to you—time, wealth, or a skill. How have you been negligent in managing it? What specific change will you make this week to be a more trustworthy steward?
?
Jihad is paired with 'good invitation.' How can you combine personal struggle against your own shortcomings with inviting others to good in a gentle way? Write down one situation where you can strive and invite simultaneously.

The First Quality: Ijtihad
Description of the first quality: knowledge at the level of ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first quality required for an imam, besides Quraysh lineage, is reaching the level of ijtihad in knowledge of religious rulings.

Core Idea
An imam must be a scholar who can derive rulings independently.

The mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith classified the qualities needed for an imam beyond lineage. The first and foremost quality is that the imam must have attained the level of ijtihad—the highest rank of juristic knowledge. This ensures he can independently derive legal rulings for new situations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see how the first quality is introduced in the context of the classification.
Notice that it is the first of three types mentioned.
2
Understand ijtihad
Reflect on what it means to be a mujtahid and why this level of knowledge is necessary for the imam to lead the community.
Ijtihad is the highest level of Islamic legal scholarship.
3
Connect to the larger classification
Understand that this is just one of three qualities, but it is given priority as the first mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوصاف من يصلح للإمامة بعد النسب من قريش]
والنوع الثاني: هو الكلام في أوصافه التي بها يصير أهلاً للإمامه عند كونه قرشيًا، قال متكلمو أهل الحديث : إن تلك الأوصاف مقسمة إلى ثلاثة أنواع: أحدها: أن يكون في العلم بالحلال والحرام وجميع الأحكام بلغ مبلغ المجتهدين.
والثاني: أن يكون عدلاً ورعًا أدنى درجاته أن يكون مقبول الشهادة. والثالث: أن يكون مهتديًا إلى وجوه السياسات والتدابير (۳) عارفاً بمراتب الناس، حافظاً لهم عليها، عالما بأسباب الحرب وتدابيرها.
Descriptions of one who is suitable for imamate after the lineage from Quraysh.
And the second type: it is the discussion of his descriptions through which he becomes qualified for imamate when he is Qurayshi. The mutakallimun of the people of hadith said: Those descriptions are divided into three types: First: that he should have reached the level of mujtahid in knowledge of the lawful and unlawful and all rulings.
Second: that he should be just and pious, the lowest degree of which is that his testimony is acceptable. Third: that he should be guided in matters of policies and administrations, knowledgeable of the ranks of people, preserving them for them, knowing the causes of war and its administrations.
Source pages p. 1117

Commentary
This passage classifies the qualities that, in addition to Quraysh lineage, are considered necessary for an imam according to the mutakallimun of the people of hadith. They divide these qualities into three categories: (1) high level of juristic knowledge (ijtihad), (2) moral integrity ('adala and wara'), and (3) administrative and military competence. The author then notes the minimal standard for each: for justice, acceptability of testimony; for political skill, knowledge of people's ranks and warfare.

Key Terms
مجتهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first quality required for an imam after Quraysh lineage?
A) Justice
B) Ijtihad
C) Political acumen
D) Generosity

Key Takeaway
The first quality of an imam according to mutakallimi ahl al-hadith is that he must be a mujtahid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you personally feel the weight of needing to understand the deen at a deep level, or are you comfortable leaving that to others? How does this passage challenge your attitude toward acquiring knowledge?
?
If you were to be in a position of leadership, would you measure up to the standard of ijtihad required for an imam? What one step can you take today to move closer to that level?
?
Consider the imams and leaders you look up to: do they possess this quality of ijtihad? If not, does that concern you, and what can you do to support the revival of qualified leadership in your community?

The Second Quality: Justice and Piety
Description of the second quality: justice and piety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the second quality required for an imam is being just and pious, with the minimum acceptable level being that his testimony is admissible in court.

Core Idea
An imam must be morally upright to be trusted.

After knowledge, the mutakallimi ahl al-hadith emphasize moral integrity. The second quality is that the imam must be 'adil (just) and wari' (pious), to the extent that his testimony is accepted. This ensures his decisions are guided by righteousness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the second quality.
Notice the minimal standard mentioned.
2
Understand 'adala and wara'
Reflect on what these terms mean in Islamic ethics and why they are essential for leadership.
'Adala' is often defined as a state of moral probity that makes one's testimony acceptable.
3
Note the minimum standard
The passage gives a pragmatic measure: the imam's testimony must be acceptable. Why do you think this is used as a benchmark?
It indicates a basic level of trustworthiness.

The Third Quality: Political and Military Competence
Description of the third quality: political and military skills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the third quality required for an imam is administrative and military expertise, including knowledge of people's ranks and warfare.

Core Idea
An imam must be skilled in governance and war.

The third quality concerns practical leadership. The mutakallimi ahl al-hadith require the imam to be guided in political strategies, understand the hierarchy of people, and be knowledgeable about military tactics. This ensures he can effectively manage the affairs of the state and defend it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third quality.
Notice it includes multiple components.
2
Break down the components
Identify the different aspects: policies, administrations, knowledge of ranks, warfare.
3
Reflect on necessity
Think about why these skills are essential for leading the Muslim community.
An imam must be able to run the state and protect it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوصاف من يصلح للإمامة بعد النسب من قريش]
والنوع الثاني: هو الكلام في أوصافه التي بها يصير أهلاً للإمامه عند كونه قرشيًا، قال متكلمو أهل الحديث : إن تلك الأوصاف مقسمة إلى ثلاثة أنواع: أحدها: أن يكون في العلم بالحلال والحرام وجميع الأحكام بلغ مبلغ المجتهدين.
والثاني: أن يكون عدلاً ورعًا أدنى درجاته أن يكون مقبول الشهادة. والثالث: أن يكون مهتديًا إلى وجوه السياسات والتدابير (۳) عارفاً بمراتب الناس، حافظاً لهم عليها، عالما بأسباب الحرب وتدابيرها.
Descriptions of one who is suitable for imamate after the lineage from Quraysh.
And the second type: it is the discussion of his descriptions through which he becomes qualified for imamate when he is Qurayshi. The mutakallimun of the people of hadith said: Those descriptions are divided into three types: First: that he should have reached the level of mujtahid in knowledge of the lawful and unlawful and all rulings.
Second: that he should be just and pious, the lowest degree of which is that his testimony is acceptable. Third: that he should be guided in matters of policies and administrations, knowledgeable of the ranks of people, preserving them for them, knowing the causes of war and its administrations.
Source pages p. 1117

Commentary
This passage classifies the qualities that, in addition to Quraysh lineage, are considered necessary for an imam according to the mutakallimun of the people of hadith. They divide these qualities into three categories: (1) high level of juristic knowledge (ijtihad), (2) moral integrity ('adala and wara'), and (3) administrative and military competence. The author then notes the minimal standard for each: for justice, acceptability of testimony; for political skill, knowledge of people's ranks and warfare.

Abu Mansur's List of Imamate Qualities: Desirable vs Necessary
Al-Nasafi distinguishes desirable imamate qualities from necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between desirable qualities for an imam and necessary conditions for the validity of the imamate, and the basis for this distinction in Maturidi thought.

Core Idea
Not all good qualities are conditions; the imamate remains valid even if some are lacking.

In this passage, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi presents and then qualifies the extensive list of imamate qualities proposed by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He argues that while these qualities are highly desirable, they are not all essential for the imamate to be valid, drawing an analogy with a judge who remains in office despite injustice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the qualities
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the many qualities Abu Mansur mentions for an imam. Notice the range from knowledge to military skill.
The list includes both religious and administrative attributes.
2
Identify the author's main claim
Find where the author distinguishes between what is 'desirable' (marghub fihi) and what is a 'condition' (shart) for the imamate. This is the core of his argument.
He uses the phrase 'fadhalika kulluhu marghubun fihi matlubun fi al-imam'.
3
Understand the analogy of the judge
Reflect on the analogy: an unjust judge is not automatically dismissed. How does this support the author's point about the imam?
The author argues by analogy: if a judge retains his position despite injustice, the imam can retain his despite lacking some qualities.
4
Connect to historical evidence
The author notes that most caliphs after the Rightly Guided Caliphs did not reach the level of ijtihad, yet their imamate was accepted. This reinforces the argument.
This shows that the community's acceptance of imams with less than ideal qualities is a practical precedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله الله - في في أثناء كلامه في حكمة تخصيص الشرع الأئمة أنهم من قريش، وقصر الأمر في ذلك عليهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون جامعا بين علم الأحكام والحلال والحرام، ومعاشرة الناس ومعاملتهم، وعلو الهمة، وصرف النفس عن الخبائث والطمع وبسط اليد في الأموال، والعفة عن الفروج والأبضاع والعدالة والورع، وبين قوة الصريعة وشدة الشكيمة، والقدرة على إنصاف المظلوم من الظالم، ورباطة الجأش والشجاعة والإقدام، وحسن القيام بتدابير الحروب وجر العساكر، والرفق في الإيالة، والقيام بأسباب السياسة، وغير ذلك مما اجتمع في أثناء كلامه، فذلك كله (۱) مرغوب فيه مطلوب في الإمام. فأما كون هذه الأشياء بأجمعها شرطًا لانعقاد الإمامة له وصيرورته أهلاً لها، فذلك غير ثابت عنه، ولم أقف عليه عن أحد (۲) من أصحابنا والرواية عن أصحابنا ظاهرة أن القاضي إذا جار لا ينعزل، وإن استحق العزل بل بقي قاضيًا، فكذا هذا (۳) ينبغي أن يكون كذلك في الإمام الأعظم. فيكون المختار من استجمع ما ذكرنا من الخصال، فأما وجودها فيه ليس بشرط لانعقاد الخلافة، وأكثر الخلفاء بعد الخلفاء الراشدين ليكونوا بلغوا في العلم مبلغ الاجتهاد، ومع ذلك اعتقد الناس صحة إمامتهم وافتراض طاعاتهم.
As for what Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in the course of his speech about the wisdom of the Law's specification of the imams being from Quraysh and restricting the matter to them—that he should be combining knowledge of rulings, lawful and unlawful, and interaction with people and their transactions, and high ambition, and turning the soul away from impurities and greed and generosity with wealth, and modesty from private parts and conjugal rights, and justice and piety, and strong will and firm resolve, and ability to give justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, and composure, courage, and boldness, and good execution of war plans and leading armies, and gentleness in governance, and undertaking the means of politics, and other things gathered in his speech—all of that is desirable and sought in the imam. As for these things collectively being a condition for the validity of imamate for him and his becoming qualified for it, that is not established from him, and I have not come across it from any of our companions. And the narration from our companions is manifest that if a judge is unjust he is not dismissed, even if he deserves dismissal; rather he remains a judge. So likewise this should be the case for the supreme imam. Thus the chosen one is he who combines the qualities we have mentioned, but their existence in him is not a condition for the validity of the caliphate. Most of the caliphs after the Rightly Guided Caliphs did not reach the level of ijtihad in knowledge, yet people believed in the soundness of their imamate and the obligation of obedience to them.
Source pages p. 1117

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi on the qualities of an imam. Al-Maturidi listed many desirable attributes, but the author (Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi) clarifies that these are not all necessary conditions for the imamate to be valid. He argues that since an unjust judge is not automatically dismissed, the same principle applies to the imam: deficiency in some qualities does not invalidate his leadership. Historical evidence supports this, as many caliphs after the Four Rightly Guided Caliphs lacked ijtihad, yet their imamate was accepted. Thus, the ideal imam possesses many qualities, but only some are truly conditions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that the listed qualities are important for the imam.
Correction: The author affirms that all these qualities are desirable (marghub fihi) but emphasizes that they are not all necessary conditions (shart) for the validity of the imamate.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The imamate remains valid even if the imam lacks some recommended qualities, as long as the core conditions are met.

?
When you consider your own role as a leader (even in a small way), are you paralyzed by the thought that you don't have enough qualities? Does this lesson free you to lead with what you have, or does it challenge you to strive for more? What would change if you focused on fulfilling necessary conditions rather than meeting an ideal?

The Essential Condition for Imamate: Political and Executive Capability
The author argues that administrative and military capability is the essential condition for imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the imam's primary qualification is political and executive capability, not necessarily personal ijtihad.

Core Idea
An imam leads, not by his scholarship, but by his strength and justice.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Sunni political theology: the qualifications of an imam versus a judge. The author argues that while a judge may rely on others' knowledge, the imam must possess practical leadership abilities to fulfill his role. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between the judge and the imam.
2
Identify the two comparisons
The author first says a judge does not need to be a mujtahid, then says the imam must be politically strong and capable. Compare the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the purpose of the imam
The author states that if the imam lacks these capabilities, the purpose for which he was appointed would not be achieved. What is that purpose?
Purpose: enforcing justice, defending borders, leading armies.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Many assume the imam must be a scholar (mujtahid). This passage clarifies that leadership capability is paramount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه : أن كون القاضي مجتهدًا ليس بشرط لما أنه يمكنه أن يقضي بعلم غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الأمر في الإمام على هذا (٤) ، فأما كونه سائسًا قويًا قادرًا على تنفيذ الأحكام وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم وسد الثغور وحماية البيضة وحفظ حدود دار الإسلام وجر (٥) العساكر، فينبغي أن يكون شرطًا إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لم يحصل ما نصب الإمام لأجله.
He verifies it: The fact that the judge is a mujtahid is not a condition because it is possible for him to judge by the knowledge of another. So the matter with the imam should be like this. However, his being a politician, strong, capable of executing judgments, giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, closing the borders, protecting the flock, preserving the frontiers of the Abode of Islam, and leading armies—this should be a condition, because if he were not like that, the purpose for which the imam was appointed would not be achieved.
Source pages p. 1118

Commentary
This passage contains a verification of the previous argument. The author distinguishes between the imam and the judge: a judge does not need to be a mujtahid because he can rely on others' knowledge. Similarly, an imam does not need scholarly ijtihad. However, the imam must possess political strength and executive capacity, because the very purpose of installing an imam is to enforce justice, defend the realm, and protect the Muslim community. Without these capabilities, the imamate fails its function.

Correction: The passage explains that while a judge can rely on others' knowledge, the imam's primary qualification is political and executive strength, not personal ijtihad.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Rafidah Claim of the Imam's Universal Knowledge
Author refutes the Rafidah notion that the imam must have universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses the Rafidah's claim that the imam must know everything, and see an extreme example used to illustrate its absurdity.

Core Idea
The Rafidah's demand for an omniscient imam is rejected as baseless and foolish.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses a controversial claim made by the Rafidah (a Shia sect) that the imam must possess comprehensive knowledge of all matters. The author dismisses this as false and absurd, providing an extreme example to highlight the unreasonableness of such a demand. Let's examine the author's concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's strong opening statement: 'قول باطل' (a false saying). This sets the tone for the refutation.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on what is present.
2
Understand the Rafidah claim and the author's response
Identify the claim: the Rafidah say the imam should know all things. The author calls this 'حماقة' (foolishness). Then an extreme example is given: some even say the imam must know how to play the lute.
The author uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the example
Ask yourself: why would the author choose such an apparently trivial skill? This shows how unreasonable the claim is when extended to its logical conclusion.
The example serves to mock the claim's absurdity.

Conditions for Imamate: Necessity vs. Desirability
Discusses conditions for imamate: not all qualities are necessary for validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the validity of the imamate does not require all desirable qualities; only practical governance abilities are essential.

Core Idea
Not every virtue is a requirement: only capable governance makes an imam valid.

This passage tackles a key debate: are all the ideal qualities of an imam actually necessary for his leadership to be legitimate? The author argues that many desirable traits, like reaching the level of ijtihad, are not conditions for validity. Instead, he shows that practical abilities to govern and protect are the real necessities. Let's see how he proves this through analogy and historical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's flow settle before interpreting.
Notice the shift from listing desirable traits to arguing they aren't conditions.
2
Identify desirable vs. necessary qualities
Find which qualities the author says are desirable but not necessary, and which he insists are essential.
The desirable ones are the virtues mentioned earlier; the essential ones are practical governance abilities.
3
Analyze the judge analogy
Consider why the author uses the example of an unjust judge who remains in office. How does this support the main argument?
The analogy shows that lack of a desirable quality (justice) does not invalidate the position.
4
Understand the historical evidence and conclusion
Read the part about caliphs after the Rightly-Guided ones and the verification about judges. See how they reinforce the distinction.
The historical example proves that ijtihad is not a condition for valid imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك مما اجتمع في أثناء كلامه، فذلك كله (١) مرغوب فيه مطلوب في الإمام. فأما كون هذه الأشياء بأجمعها شرطًا لانعقاد الإمامة له وصيرورته أهلاً لها، فذلك غير ثابت عنه، ولم أقف عليه عن أحد (٢) من أصحابنا والرواية عن أصحابنا ظاهرة أن القاضي إذا جار لا ينعزل، وإن استحق العزل بل بقي قاضيًا، فكذا هذا (٣) ينبغي أن يكون كذلك في الإمام الأعظم. فيكون المختار من استجمع ما ذكرنا من الخصال، فأما وجودها فيه ليس بشرط لانعقاد الخلافة، وأكثر الخلفاء بعد الخلفاء الراشدين ليكونوا بلغوا في العلم مبلغ الاجتهاد، ومع ذلك اعتقد الناس صحة إمامتهم وافتراض طاعاتهم.
يحققه : أن كون القاضي مجتهدًا ليس بشرط لما أنه يمكنه أن يقضي بعلم غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الأمر في الإمام على هذا (٤) ، فأما كونه سائسًا قويًا قادرًا على تنفيذ الأحكام وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم وسد الثغور وحماية البيضة وحفظ حدود دار الإسلام وجر (٥) العساكر، فينبغي أن يكون شرطًا إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لم يحصل ما نصب الإمام لأجله.
That is among what is gathered in the midst of his speech, so all of that (1) is desirable and sought for the Imam. As for these things collectively being a condition for the validity of the Imamate for him and his becoming worthy of it, that is not established from him, and I have not come across it from any (2) of our companions. And the transmitted report from our companions is apparent that if a judge is unjust, he is not removed, even if he deserves removal, but rather he remains a judge. So likewise this (3) should be the case for the supreme Imam. Thus the chosen one is he who combines the qualities we mentioned; but their existence in him is not a condition for the validity of the Caliphate. And most of the Caliphs after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs did not reach the level of ijtihad in knowledge, yet people believed in the validity of their Imamate and the obligation of obedience to them.
He verifies: That the judge being a mujtahid is not a condition, because he can judge based on another's knowledge. So the matter of the Imam should be upon this (4). As for him being a capable, strong administrator able to execute judgments, give justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, secure the frontiers, protect the territory, guard the borders of the Abode of Islam, and lead (5) armies, then that should be a condition, for if he were not like that, what the Imam was appointed for would not be achieved.
Source pages p. 1118

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of imamate, distinguishing between desirable qualities and actual requirements for validity. The author begins by stating that all the previously mentioned qualities are desirable in an Imam. However, he argues that having them all is not a necessary condition for the Imamate to be valid. He supports this by citing that none of the Hanafi scholars hold that all these qualities are prerequisites. He draws an analogy with a judge: a judge who becomes unjust is not automatically removed; he remains a judge despite deserving removal. Similarly, an Imam who lacks some desirable qualities still holds a valid Imamate. The author then notes that many caliphs after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs did not reach the level of ijtihad, yet their Imamate was accepted and obedience to them was considered obligatory. He further verifies by explaining that a judge need not be a mujtahid, as he can rely on others' knowledge. This applies to the Imam as well. However, the author emphasizes that certain practical qualities—like being a strong administrator, capable of executing judgments, ensuring justice, securing frontiers, and leading armies—are indeed necessary conditions. Without these, the purpose of appointing an Imam (governance and protection) cannot be fulfilled.

Key Terms
إمامة
شرط
قاض
اجتهاد
خلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All virtues of an Imam are necessary for the validity of the Imamate.
Correction: Only practical governance abilities are essential; other virtues like ijtihad are desirable but not required for validity.

Checkpoint

B) Because removal would cause greater harm.
C) Because unjust judges are still considered valid by default.
D) The passage does not give a reason.

Key Takeaway
The imamate is valid as long as the leader can effectively govern and protect; other virtues like ijtihad are commendable but not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of Rawafid Claim on Imam's Knowledge
Refutes the Rawafid claim that the imam must know all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Rawafid claim that the Imam must know all things is false and entails disbelief.

Core Idea
Requiring the Imam to know all elevates the Caliphate above prophethood—pure kufr.

The Rawafid (Shi'a) assert that the Imam must possess comprehensive knowledge, even of the unseen and trivial matters. This lesson examines the Maturidi refutation, which exposes the absurdity of this claim and demonstrates that it contradicts the very nature of the Imamate as a succession of prophethood. The author uses strong language to dismiss the argument and then provides a logical and scriptural proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the author's tone and structure.
Note the author's dismissal of the Rawafid as foolish.
2
Identify the Rawafid claims
Find the specific claims attributed to the Rawafid: that the Imam must know all things, including playing the lute, and that he must know the unseen.
Also note the absurd example of the Imam's dog being infallible.
3
Understand the author's response
The author first dismisses these claims as ignorant and advises avoiding debate. Then he provides a principled argument: Imamate is succession of prophethood.
4
Analyze the hadith argument
The author cites the hadith where the Prophet says he knows religious affairs better, and people know worldly affairs better. This shows that requiring the Imam to know worldly affairs or the unseen would elevate the Caliphate above prophethood, which is kufr.
This is the key theological refutation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the author calls such a requirement pure disbelief. Connect it to the concept of imamate as succession.
This lesson establishes a critical boundary in Sunni doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الروافض في شمول علم الإمام قول باطل] ولا معنى لحماقة الروافض أن الإمام ينبغي أن يكون عالما بكل الأمور، حتى ألزم بعضهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون عالمًا بضرب (٦) العود فالتزم ذلك، وحكى عن بعضهم (١) أن الإمام ينبغي أن يعلم الغيب، وهذا كما حكي عن بعضهم ينبغي أن يكون كلب الإمام معصوماً وهذا (٢) تجاهل، ومن بلغ في الغباء هذه الغاية يعرض عن مناظرته.
ثم علم الإمام يحتاج إليه لتنفيذ أمور الرعية، فما وراء ذلك من العلوم لا معنى لاشتراطه.
يحققه أن الإمامة خلافة النبوة والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "أنا أعلم(٣) بأمور دينكم وأنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم (٤) فمن شرط أن يكون الإمام عالمًا بأمور الدنيا أو يعلم الغيب فقد جعل درجة الخلافة أعلى من درجة النبوة، وهذا كفر محض.
[The saying of the Rawafid concerning the comprehensiveness of the Imam's knowledge is a false statement] And there is no meaning to the foolishness of the Rawafid that the Imam should be knowledgeable of all things, to the extent that some of them insisted that he should be knowledgeable of playing the lute (6), and they adhered to that. And it is reported from some of them (1) that the Imam should know the unseen, and this is like what is reported from some of them: that the Imam's dog should be infallible. And this (2) is ignorance, and whoever has reached this limit of stupidity, one should avoid debating with him.
Then, the Imam's knowledge is needed for administering the affairs of the subjects; what is beyond that of sciences has no meaning in stipulating it.
He verifies: That the Imamate is the succession of prophethood, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "I am more knowing(3) of your religious affairs, and you are more knowing of your worldly affairs (4)." So whoever stipulates that the Imam be knowledgeable of worldly affairs or know the unseen has made the degree of the Caliphate higher than the degree of prophethood, and this is pure disbelief.
Source pages p. 1118

Commentary
This passage refutes the Rawafid (Shi'a) claim that the Imam must have comprehensive knowledge. The author begins by declaring the Rawafid's view on the Imam's all-encompassing knowledge as false. He dismisses their idea as foolishness, citing extreme examples: some Rawafid insisted the Imam must know how to play the lute, and others claimed the Imam should know the unseen—comparing it to the absurd notion that the Imam's dog should be infallible. He advises avoiding debate with such people due to their extreme ignorance. The author then clarifies that the knowledge required of the Imam is only that needed to administer the subjects' affairs; demanding knowledge beyond that is meaningless. He verifies this by invoking the principle that the Imamate is a succession of prophethood. He cites the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said he is more knowledgeable of religious affairs while the people are more knowledgeable of worldly affairs. Thus, requiring the Imam to know worldly affairs or the unseen would elevate the Caliphate above prophethood, which is pure disbelief (kufr).

الروافض
علم الإمام
الغيب
خلافة النبوة
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam must know all things, including the unseen and minor worldly skills.
Correction: The Imam only needs knowledge for administering affairs; requiring beyond contradicts prophetic succession and constitutes kufr.

Checkpoint

A) It is acceptable as long as the Imam is capable.
B) It makes the Caliphate equal to prophethood.
C) It makes the Caliphate higher than prophethood, which is pure disbelief.
D) It is a minor sin.

Key Takeaway
Requiring the Imam to know worldly affairs or the unseen contradicts the succession of prophethood and constitutes disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Must the Imam Be the Best? Maturidi vs. Ash'ari
Two positions on imam being the best

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on whether the Imam must be the most excellent person of his time.

Core Idea
The Maturidis allow a virtuous imam, even if someone better exists.

This passage presents a key doctrinal difference between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding the Imamate. While some insist only the best can lead, the Maturidis maintain that virtue and suitability suffice. The author cites supporters and then introduces the opposing Ash'ari view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Note the two positions presented.
The passage is cut off at the end but the core arguments are clear.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the Maturidi view (not a condition) and the Ash'ari view (necessary condition). Understand the difference.
The Maturidi position is supported by a list of scholars and groups.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this difference matters. How does each view affect the legitimacy of an imam?
This connects to broader concepts of leadership and qualifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون الإمام أفضل أهل زمانه ليس بشرط] فأما كونه أفضل أهل زمانه ليس بشرط عندنا نص عليه الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في كتاب المقالات، بل إذا كان فاضلاً صالحًا للإمامة وعقدت له الإمامة انعقدت، وإن كان الأفضل منه موجودًا، وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة والحسين بن الفضل البجلي (٥) من متكلمي أهل الحديث وأبو العباس القلانسي ومحمد بن إسحق بن خزيمة (٦).
قول الأشاعرة في الأفضلية] وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري بأن إمامة المفضول لا تنعقد مع وجود الفاضل، ويقول: إن من الأوصاف التي يصير بها المرء أهلاً للإمامة أن لا يكون أحد من ...
[That the Imam be the best of his time is not a condition] As for him being the best of his time, it is not a condition according to us, as stated by Shaykh Abu Mansur - may Allah have mercy on him - in the Book of Articles. Rather, if he is virtuous and suitable for the Imamate, and the Imamate is contracted for him, it is valid, even if there is one better than him. And this is the view of the Mu'tazila, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali (5) among the theologians of the People of Hadith, Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma (6).
[The saying of the Ash'aris concerning the best] And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari holds that the Imamate of the inferior is not valid in the presence of the superior. And he says: Among the descriptions by which a person becomes worthy of the Imamate is that there should be no one ...
Source pages p. 1119

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on whether the Imam must be the most excellent (afdal) of his time, followed by the contrasting Ash'ari view. The author begins by stating that according to the Maturidi school, it is not a condition for the Imam to be the best—this is explicitly affirmed by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi in his book al-Maqalat. The author explains that if a person is virtuous and suitable for the Imamate, and the oath of allegiance is given to him, the Imamate is valid even if a better person exists. He then lists supporters of this view: the Mu'tazila, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali (a theologian of the People of Hadith), Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma. Next, the Ash'ari position is introduced: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari holds that the Imamate of an inferior person does not contract in the presence of a superior one. He argues that among the qualities that make one worthy of the Imamate is that there be no one better (the text cuts off).

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school holds that the imam need not be the best of his time; virtue and suitability are sufficient.

Why the Imam Need Not Know the Unseen
The author refutes the claim that the imam must know the unseen and worldly affairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why demanding that the imam know the unseen or worldly affairs is both irrational and theologically problematic, as it would elevate the caliphate above prophethood.

Core Idea
Demanding the imam know the unseen contradicts the nature of imamate as succession to prophethood.

Some groups have made extreme demands regarding the knowledge required of the imam, such as knowing the unseen. The author of this passage dismisses such demands as not only unnecessary but also dangerous to the fabric of Islamic belief. By comparing them to absurd claims and grounding his argument in the Prophet's own words, he shows how such conditions undermine the very concept of khilafa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the absurd comparison.
Start by reading the Arabic passage along with its translation. Notice how the author compares the claim to the saying that the imam's dog must be infallible. This sets the tone for the refutation.
The comparison is meant to show the claim's foolishness.
2
Understand the principle: imamate needs only practical knowledge.
The author states that the imam's knowledge is needed only for carrying out the affairs of the subjects. Reflect on why this distinction is important.
This limits the scope of required knowledge to practical matters.
3
Grasp the theological consequence: elevating khilafa above nubuwwa is kufr.
The author uses the Prophet's hadith to argue that requiring worldly or unseen knowledge would place the caliphate above prophethood, which is pure disbelief. Consider the gravity of this conclusion.
This ties the argument to the foundational belief in the superiority of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه قال: بعضهم أن الإمام ينبغي أن يعلم الغيب، وهذا كما حكي عن بعضهم ينبغي أن يكون كلب الإمام معصوماً وهذا تجاهل، ومن بلغ في الغباء هذه الغاية يعرض عن مناظرته. ثم علم الإمام يحتاج إليه لتنفيذ أمور الرعية، فما وراء ذلك من العلوم لا معنى لاشتراطه. يحققه أن الإمامة خلافة النبوة والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "أنا أعلم بأمور دينكم وأنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم" فمن شرط أن يكون الإمام عالمًا بأمور الدنيا أو يعلم الغيب فقد جعل درجة الخلافة أعلى من درجة النبوة، وهذا كفر محض.
He said: Some of them [hold] that the imam should know the unseen. And this is like what is narrated from some of them: the imam's dog should be infallible. And this is ignorance. And whoever reaches this limit of foolishness, one should refrain from debating him. Then, the imam's knowledge is needed for carrying out the affairs of the subjects; whatever sciences are beyond that, there is no meaning to stipulating them. He confirms this by [saying] that the imamate is the succession of prophethood, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'I know best about your religious affairs, and you know best about your worldly affairs.' So whoever stipulates that the imam be knowledgeable about worldly affairs or know the unseen has made the degree of the caliphate higher than the degree of prophethood, and this is pure disbelief.
Source pages p. 1119

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the imam must know the unseen or worldly affairs. The author begins by comparing such a claim to the absurd idea that the imam's dog must be infallible, showing it is ignorance. He then states that the only knowledge required of an imam is that needed to execute the affairs of the subjects; other sciences need not be stipulated. He confirms this by noting that imamate is the succession of prophethood, and the Prophet himself distinguished between his role in religious affairs and the people's expertise in worldly matters. Therefore, demanding that the imam know worldly affairs or the unseen would elevate the caliphate above prophethood, which is pure disbelief.

Correction: The imam only needs knowledge to execute the affairs of the subjects; demanding more elevates khilafa above prophethood, which is kufr.

C) The imam must be from Quraysh
D) The imam must be the most excellent

Key Takeaway
Setting impossible knowledge conditions for the imam contradicts the nature of khilafa and leads to kufr.

6 min
The Imam Need Not Be the Most Excellent of His Time
The author states the Maturidi view and refutes the Ash'ari objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the imam does not have to be the most excellent person of his time, and the evidence used to support this view against the Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
Imamate is about practical benefit, not abstract superiority.

This passage presents a key difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris on the qualifications for the imam. While the Ash'aris insist the imam must be the most excellent person, the Maturidis argue that capability and benefit for the community are more important. The author cites the precedent of 'Umar's shura council to demonstrate that even the Companions did not require absolute superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is dense but covers a complete argument.
2
Identify the two conflicting positions
Notice how the author first states the Maturidi view (imam need not be most excellent) and then the Ash'ari view (imam must be most excellent). List the groups that support each side.
3
Examine the Ash'ari arguments
The Ash'aris give two reasons: the more excellent inspires obedience, and analogy with prophethood. Understand why they consider this a condition.
4
Understand the Maturidi rebuttal
The author uses 'Umar's shura council and the ongoing practice of appointing less excellent caliphs. Also, true superiority is known only to Allah, so basing imamate on it is impractical.
Reflect on how historical examples outweigh theoretical analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما كونه أفضل أهل زمانه ليس بشرط عندنا نص عليه الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في كتاب المقالات، بل إذا كان فاضلاً صالحاً للإمامة وعقدت له الإمامة انعقدت، وإن كان الأفضل منه موجوداً، وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة والحسين بن الفضل البجلي من متكلمي أهل الحديث وأبو العباس القلانسي ومحمد بن إسحق بن خزيمة. وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري بأن إمامة المفضول لا تنعقد مع وجود الفاضل، ويقول: إن من الأوصاف التي يصير بها المرء أهلاً للإمامة أن لا يكون أحد من أهل زمانه أفضل منه، فإذا وجد الأفضل لم يثبت للمفضول أهلية الإمامة، ويقولون أيضا إن الأفضل أقرب إلى انقياد النفوس إلى متابعته، وإيثار طاعته والائتمار لأوامره (أمكن) (وأميل) ويقولون : إن الإمامة يطلب فيها الفضل فيجب أن يطلب لها على رتبة فيه قياساً على النبوة. إلا أن أصحابنا - رحمهم الله - احتجوا أن عمر لما طعن الله جعل الخلافة شورى بين ستة نفر مع ظهور فضيلة بعضهم على بعض، ولم يعين الأفضل منهم بل فوض إليهم ليختاروا من كانت المصلحة بإمامته أعم للخلق، ومن كان أقدر على القيام بما فوض القيام به إلى الإمام وإن كان غيره أفضل منه في نفسه، وهذا لأن كون عثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهما - أفضل ممن دخل معهما في الشورى كان ظاهراً إنما الالتباس كان في أن قيام أيهم بأمور الأمة ومصالح المسلمين أنفع لهم وأعود وأعم فائدة وأتم عائدة، ففوض إليهم ذلك لينظروا فيه فيقلدوا الإمامة أصلحهم لذلك وكذا بعد الخلفاء الراشدين إلى يومنا هذا عقدت الخلافة لإنسان، وفي قريش من هو أفضل منه وأورع وأتقى وأعلم، ورأى الناس إمامهم في كل زمان مفترض الطاعة على أن لا وجه لمعرفة فضيلة أحد على طريق الحقيقة عند الله تعالى، بل هو أمر يثبت بالاجتهاد وغالب الظن فيه فتعليق الحكم به تعليق بما لا يمكن القيام به والحاجة إلى معرفة ذلك بالناس ماسة، إذ الخلافة تثبت بعقدهم واختيارهم، فإذا كان لا يمكنهم الوقوف على ذلك حقيقة لم يكن
As for him being the most excellent of his time, it is not a condition with us. Sheikh Abu Mansur — may Allah have mercy on him — explicitly stated this in the Book of Articles. Rather, if he is virtuous, suitable for the imamate, and the imamate is contracted for him, it becomes contracted, even if one more excellent than him exists. And the Mu'tazila, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali among the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith, Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma went to this [view]. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that the imamate of the less excellent does not contract in the presence of the more excellent. He says: Among the attributes by which a person becomes qualified for the imamate is that there be no one of his time more excellent than him. So when the more excellent is found, the less excellent does not have qualification for the imamate. They also say that the more excellent is closer to the submission of souls to following him, preferring his obedience, and acting upon his commands (possible) (and more inclined). And they say: Excellence is sought in the imamate, so it must be sought at a rank on it, by analogy with prophethood. However, our companions — may Allah have mercy on them — argued that when 'Umar was stabbed (may Allah be pleased with him), he made the caliphate a consultation (shura) among six persons, despite the manifest excellence of some over others. He did not appoint the most excellent among them, but delegated to them to choose whomever had the benefit of his imamate more general for the creation, and whoever was more capable of undertaking what was delegated to the imam, even if another was more excellent than him in himself. This is because it was apparent that 'Uthman and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) were more excellent than those who entered the shura with them; the confusion was only about which of them would be most beneficial for the affairs of the community and the interests of the Muslims, most useful, most general in benefit, and most complete in return. So he delegated that to them to consider it and entrust the imamate to the most suitable for it. Likewise, after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs up to our present day, the caliphate has been contracted to a person while among Quraysh there were those more excellent, more pious, more God-fearing, and more knowledgeable. People have seen their imam in every time as one whose obedience is obligatory, and there is no way to know the superiority of anyone in reality with Allah, for it is a matter established by ijtihad and predominant opinion. So tying the ruling to it would be tying it to something that cannot be undertaken, and the need to know that is pressing for the people, since the caliphate is established by their contract and choice. So if they cannot ascertain it in reality, it would not be...
Source pages p. 1119

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi view that the imam does not have to be the best person of his time. It first states the position with the authority of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and lists other groups who agree, including the Mu'tazila and certain scholars. Then it presents the Ash'ari opposing view, which holds that a less excellent person cannot become imam when a more excellent one exists, and gives two reasons: the more excellent is more likely to inspire obedience, and by analogy with prophethood where the best is chosen. The author then provides the Maturidi proof, which is based on historical precedent: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab appointed a council of six, including 'Uthman and 'Ali who were clearly more excellent than some others, yet he did not specify the best but left it to consultation. This shows that the benefit for the community is more important than absolute individual superiority. Furthermore, throughout history, caliphs have been appointed while there were more excellent individuals among Quraysh. Also, true superiority is known only to Allah, so making imamate conditional on it would be impractical. Therefore, the condition is rejected.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الأفضلية
المفضول
الشورى
الخلافة
أهل الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the imam must always be the most excellent person of his time.
Correction: The Maturidis show that practical benefit and capability can outweigh individual superiority, using the shura of 'Umar and historical practice.

Checkpoint
What historical event does the author use to prove that the imam need not be the most excellent?
A) The appointment of Abu Bakr
B) The shura of six after 'Umar's assassination
C) The Treaty of Hudaybiyya
D) The Battle of Badr

Key Takeaway
The imam is chosen based on capability and benefit for the community, not solely on individual excellence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at those in authority over you (parents, teachers, leaders), do you focus on their flaws compared to others, or on their ability to fulfill their role? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to compare?
?
This lesson argues that practical benefit outweighs abstract superiority. Where in your life do you prioritize pride in having the 'best' over actually benefiting the community? What would change if you reversed that?
?
The passage says we cannot truly know who is best in Allah's sight. Are you comfortable with that uncertainty? Does it free you from judging others, or do you still secretly rank people? Repent of that.

Refutation of the Condition of Superiority for the Imam
Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari condition that the imam must be the most excellent of his time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī argument that the imam need not be the most excellent of his time, and how historical precedent and epistemological limits support this view.

Core Idea
Leadership in Islam prioritizes capability and public benefit over personal excellence.

This passage tackles a key point of contention between Ash'ari and Māturīdī thought: must the imam be the absolute best person in his era? The author first presents the Ash'ari argument that excellence is necessary, then delivers a powerful rebuttal grounded in the precedent of Umar's shura council and a sharp distinction between human knowledge and divine selection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari argument
Identify the three points the opponents make: (1) if a more excellent person exists, the less excellent is disqualified, (2) the more excellent is more likely to be obeyed, (3) imamate requires excellence by analogy with prophethood.
Note that they claim direct analogy between imamate and prophethood.
2
Understand the Māturīdī response using Umar's shura
The author cites Umar's appointment of a six-person consultative council (shura) despite clear differences in excellence. Umar did not appoint the most excellent but left it to the council to choose based on maslaha (public benefit) and capability.
This historical precedent directly challenges the necessity of afdaliyya.
3
Analyze the epistemological argument
The passage argues that true excellence relative to Allah is known only by Him; humans can only form opinions through ijtihad. Attaching the imamate to something unknowable would make the selection impractical.
This distinguishes imamate from prophethood, where Allah directly chooses.
4
Contrast with prophethood
The author emphasizes that prophethood is different because Allah chooses the prophet with full knowledge. For imamate, humans choose, so they cannot be held to the same standard of knowing absolute excellence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل زمانه أفضل منه، فإذا وجد الأفضل لم يثبت للمفضول أهلية الإمامة، ويقولون أيضا إن الأفضل أقرب إلى انقياد النفوس إلى متابعته، وإيثار طاعته والائتمار لأوامره (أمكن) (وأميل)، ويقولون : إن الإمامة يطلب فيها الفضل فيجب أن يطلب لها على رتبة فيه قياسًا على النبوة. حجتنا في عدم الأفضلية: إلا أن أصحابنا - رحمهم الله - احتجوا أن عمر لما طعن الله جعل الخلافة شورى بين ستة نفر مع ظهور فضيلة بعضهم على بعض، ولم يعين الأفضل منهم بل فوض إليهم ليختاروا من كانت المصلحة بإمامته أعم للخلق، ومن كان أقدر على القيام بما فوض القيام به إلى الإمام وإن كان غيره أفضل منه في نفسه، وهذا لأن كون عثمان وعلي - رضي الله عنهما - أفضل ممن دخل معهما في الشورى كان ظاهرا إنما الالتباس كان في أن قيام أيهم بأمور الأمة ومصالح المسلمين أنفع لهم وأعود وأعم فائدة وأتم عائدة، ففوض إليهم ذلك لينظروا فيه فيقلدوا الإمامة أصلحهم لذلك وكذا بعد الخلفاء الراشدين إلى يومنا هذا عقدت الخلافة لإنسان، وفي قريش من هو أفضل منه وأورع وأتقى وأعلم، ورأى الناس إمامهم في كل زمان مفترض الطاعة على أن لا وجه لمعرفة فضيلة أحد على طريق الحقيقة عند الله تعالى، بل هو أمر يثبت بالاجتهاد وغالب الظن فيه فتعليق الحكم به تعليق بما لا يمكن القيام به والحاجة إلى معرفة ذلك بالناس ماسة، إذ الخلافة تثبت بعقدهم واختيارهم، فإذا كان لا يمكنهم الوقوف على ذلك حقيقة لم يكن لتعليق الحكم به فائدة بخلاف النبوة، فإن الله تعالى هو الذي يختار من يشاء من عباده لرسالته ونبوته، وهو العالم بحقيقة كل شيء، فكان من اختاره من أهل زمانه لرسالته وتحميل أمانته أفضل خليقته وأكمل بريته في وقته، وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه، والله تعالى الموفق
The people of his time are more excellent than him, so if the more excellent is found, the less excellent does not have the qualification for the imamate. And they also say that the more excellent is closer to the compliance of souls in following him, and preferring his obedience and acting on his commands [?][?], and they say: the imamate seeks excellence, so it is obligatory to seek for it a rank of excellence by analogy with prophethood. Our proof against the necessity of superiority: our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, argued that when Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, was stabbed, he made the caliphate a consultative council among six men, even though the superiority of some over others was apparent, and he did not specify the most excellent among them, but delegated to them so they could choose one whose imamate would bring the most benefit to the creation, and who was most capable of undertaking what is delegated to the imam, even if another was more excellent than him in himself. This is because it was apparent that Uthman and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them, were more excellent than the others in the council; the ambiguity was only concerning which of them would carry out the affairs of the nation and the interests of the Muslims in a way most beneficial and complete. So he delegated that to them so they could consider and install as imam the most suitable for that. Likewise, after the Rightly Guided Caliphs up to our day, the caliphate was contracted for a person while among the Quraysh there were those more excellent, more pious, more god-fearing, and more knowledgeable than him. And the people saw their imam in every age as one whose obedience is obligatory, because there is no way to know the superiority of anyone truly with respect to Allah; rather it is a matter established by ijtihad and predominant opinion. So attaching the ruling to it is attaching it to something impossible to fulfill, and the need of the people to know that is pressing, since the caliphate is established by their contract and choice. If they cannot ascertain that truly, then attaching the ruling to it has no benefit, unlike prophethood, for Allah Almighty chooses whomever He wills from His servants for His message and prophethood, and He knows the reality of everything. So whomever He chose from the people of his time for His message and the burden of His trust was the best of His creation and the most complete of His creatures in his time. But in what we are discussing, it is the opposite. And Allah Almighty is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 1120

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari condition that the imam must be the most excellent (al-afdal) of his time. The author first states the opponent's argument: if a more excellent person exists, the less excellent is not qualified for imamate; the more excellent is more likely to be obeyed; and imamate requires excellence by analogy with prophethood. Then the author presents the Māturīdī response, using the historical precedent of Umar's shura among six companions, even though some (like Uthman and Ali) were clearly more excellent. Umar did not appoint the most excellent but left the choice to the council, focusing on public interest (maslaha) and capability. Similarly, later caliphs were appointed even when more virtuous Quraysh existed, because true virtue is known only to Allah and cannot be definitively ascertained by humans; it is a matter of ijtihad and predominant opinion. Requiring the most excellent would impose an impossible condition. Prophethood is different because Allah directly chooses the prophet and knows true excellence. Thus, for imamate, excellence is not a necessary condition.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الأفضلية
الشورى
المصلحة
الاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Māturīdīs deny any importance of virtue in an imam.
Correction: Māturīdīs deny that being the most excellent is a necessary condition for imamate; virtue is desirable but not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī argument, why did Umar appoint a shura instead of naming the most excellent person as caliph?
A) Because he did not know who was most excellent.
B) Because the more excellent might not be the most capable for the job, and maslaha (public interest) was the priority.
C) Because the Quraysh did not accept the most excellent.
D) Because he wanted to avoid conflict.

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdī position is that the imam need not be the most excellent; rather, capability and public benefit are the decisive criteria for leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate others for positions of responsibility in your life, do you secretly prefer those who appear more 'excellent' in status or piety, or do you honestly assess who is most capable and beneficial? What would it cost you to change that habit?
?
This lesson argues that we cannot truly know who is most excellent before Allah. Think of a person you have dismissed as less worthy. Could it be that they are actually better than you in Allah's sight? How does that possibility unsettle your pride?
?
If the principle of prioritizing capability and public good over personal superiority were applied to your own ambitions, what would you have to give up? Are you willing to step aside for someone more suited, even if it means losing recognition?

Why Infallibility Is Not Required for the Imam
Nasafi argues that infallibility is not required for the imam because it is a prophetic condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi scholars do not require infallibility (isma) for the imam, and how this is grounded in rational and scriptural arguments.

Core Idea
Infallibility is for prophets alone; requiring it for others is an impossible obligation.

This passage from Nasafi presents the Maturidi position that infallibility is not a condition for the imamate. He argues that isma is a divine protection granted to prophets through miracles, and imams do not possess such miracles. To require infallibility from those choosing the imam would be an obligation beyond human capacity, which Allah has negated in the Quran and reason deems repugnant. The passage concludes by contrasting the necessity of prophetic infallibility for conveying revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the key terms like 'isma' and 'imamah'.
Notice that the passage begins with a clear thesis statement.
2
Identify the main arguments
Look for two main lines of reasoning: first, that infallibility is a prophetic condition tied to miracles; second, that requiring it for the imam would be an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq).
The author uses both scriptural and rational proofs.
3
Analyze the Quranic and rational proofs
Reflect on the Quranic verse quoted and the rational argument about the impossibility of knowing infallibility without revelation. Consider why this makes the requirement unreasonable.
Contrast this with the prophet's need for infallibility in conveying revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يشترط عصمة الإمام لأن العصمة للأنبياء: وكذا كونه معصوماً ليس بشرط عندنا بل العصمة من شرط النبوة إذ هي مقترنة بأعلام، ومعجزات خارجات عن العادات والطبائع يعرف بذلك صدقهم وتظهر عصمتهم، وليس الأئمة شيء من ذلك، فلو وجب القول بعصمته لوجب مع من غير دليل، إذ لا عصمة بدون صحة السريرة وسلامة الباطن، ولا وقوف على ذلك إلا بالوحي، ولا وحي مع من يختار الإمام لعقد الإمامة وهم مأمورون بنصب الإمام على ما بينا، فلو لزمهم نصب الإمام المعصوم وليس معهم دليل عصمته فكان فيه تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، والله تعالى تبرأ عن تكليف مثله بقوله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ إِلَّا مَا ءَاتَنها وغير ذلك، وقبح ذلك أيضا في العقول، على أن النبي عليه السلام يأتي بما هو غائب عن أهل الأرض، وهو الشريعة التي أتى بها وبه ظهور ذلك، ثم يظهر ذلك فيهم بالقول تارة وبالفعل أخرى، فلو لم يكن معصوما لكذب في تبليغه أو نسق في تعاطيه، فتقبل الناس قوله بإباحة شيء هو محرم في الحقيقة إلا أنه كاذب في تبليغه إباحته، وكذا
The infallibility of the imam is not a condition because infallibility is for the prophets. Likewise, his being infallible is not a condition according to us. Rather, infallibility is a condition of prophethood, since it is accompanied by signs and miracles that transcend customs and natures, by which their truthfulness is known and their infallibility appears. The imams have none of that. So if the statement of his infallibility were obligatory, it would be obligatory without evidence, for there is no infallibility without soundness of conscience and purity of inner self, and there is no knowledge of that except through revelation. And there is no revelation for those who choose the imam for the contract of imamate, while they are commanded to appoint the imam as we have explained. If they were required to appoint an infallible imam without having evidence of his infallibility, that would impose an obligation beyond capacity. Allah Almighty has declared Himself free from imposing such an obligation by His statement: 'Allah does not charge a soul except [with that within] its capacity' [Quran 2:286] and 'except what He has given it' [Quran 65:7] and other verses. That is also deemed repugnant by reason. Moreover, the Prophet, peace be upon him, brings what is hidden from the people of the earth, and that is the law he brought, and through him it appears. Then it appears among them sometimes by speech, sometimes by action. If he were not infallible, he would lie in his conveyance or make mistakes in his actions, so the people would accept his statement permitting something that is in reality forbidden, while he is lying in his conveyance of its permission. And likewise...
Source pages p. 1121

Commentary
This passage argues that infallibility (isma) is not a condition for the imam according to the Maturidi school. The author states that isma is a condition of prophethood because it is proven by miracles that transcend natural laws. Imams do not have such miracles. Therefore requiring infallibility for the imam would be an obligation without evidence, since isma can only be known through revelation, and those who choose the imam do not have access to revelation. To require them to appoint an infallible imam without being able to know who is infallible would impose an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq), which Allah has negated in the Quran. Moreover, reason also deems it repugnant. The author then draws a contrast with the Prophet: he must be infallible because he conveys revelation; without infallibility, people might accept his statements thinking they are from Allah while he is mistaken. The argument is cut off at 'and likewise' (wa kadha).

Key Terms
عصمة
إمامة
نبوة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
معجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Maturidi rejection of imamic infallibility implies the imam is not required to be just or upright.
Correction: Infallibility in the prophetic sense is not a condition, but general probity ('adala) is still required for the imam.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that infallibility cannot be a condition for the imam?
A) Because the imam does not need to be truthful.
B) Because infallibility is known only through revelation and miracles, which imams do not possess.
C) Because the Quran explicitly forbids such a condition.
D) Because the early caliphs were not infallible.

Key Takeaway
Infallibility is a divine protection granted only to prophets; requiring it for the imam would impose an obligation beyond human capacity and is therefore not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded perfection from a religious leader, teacher, or even yourself in practicing Islam? How does Nasafi’s argument that infallibility is only for prophets challenge your expectation of flawlessness? What are you going to do this week to lower that expectation and accept human fallibility?
?
The passage that we cannot know inner purity without revelation means we often judge people’s sincerity by external signs alone. When have you judged someone’s faith or character based only on appearances? How can you guard yourself against this arrogance?
?
Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity—yet you may be burdening yourself by demanding that you never sin or make mistakes. Are you carrying a weight Allah never placed on you? What would it look like to trust in His mercy instead of your own imagined perfection?
